    {
      "title": "Disaster Recovery and Business Continuity Plans",
      "source_url": "https://cloudian.com/guides/disaster-recovery/disaster-recovery-and-business-continuity-plans/",
      "content": "Disaster Recovery (DR) plans, initially focused on natural disasters in the 1970s, evolved into Business Continuity (BC) plans from the 1980s onwards. DR narrowly focuses on restoring IT systems and data after a disruption, aiming to minimize downtime and data loss. A DR plan includes recovery objectives (RTO/RPO), backup procedures, recovery environments, roles, step-by-step recovery instructions, and testing. BC, on the other hand, is a broader, proactive process ensuring the entire organization continues operating during and after a crisis, covering business processes, human resources, partners, and suppliers. A BC plan details how a business will maintain financial viability, market position, reputation, and customers. A typical BC plan has 7 chapters: goals, budget, personnel, Business Impact Analysis, proactive strategies, immediate reactive strategies (including IT DR), and long-term reactive strategies. The key differences lie in scope (organization-wide vs. IT-focused), objective (maintaining operations vs. restoring systems), timing (proactive vs. reactive), business areas covered (people, processes, partners vs. technology, data), responsibility (executive/cross-functional vs. IT/security), metrics (business tolerance vs. technical targets), and output (operational vs. technical procedures). DR is a subset of BC, providing the technical foundation for maintaining operations. Cloudian offers solutions for data continuity, including low-cost disk-based storage up to 1.5 Petabytes and replication to cloud services like Amazon S3, Azure Blob Storage, or Google Cloud Storage.",

      "content": "Business Continuity Disaster Recovery (BCDR) is a process to help organizations resume normal operations after a disaster. While related, Business Continuity Plans (BCPs) are proactive, focusing on maintaining operations before, during, and immediately after a disaster, whereas Disaster Recovery Plans (DRPs) are reactive, focusing on IT system recovery post-incident. Both rely on Recovery Time Objective (RTO), the time to restore business processes, and Recovery Point Objective (RPO), the amount of data an organization can afford to lose. Key steps in BCDR planning include Business Impact Analysis (BIA) to identify risks and their potential impact, planning responses to threats, defining roles and responsibilities, and continuous testing and refinement. DRPs also involve risk analysis and asset inventory. Companies worldwide were projected to spend USD 219 billion on cybersecurity in 2023, a 12% increase from the previous year.     {
      "title": "investopedia.com/terms/d/debtequityratio.asp",
      "source_url": "https://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/debtequityratio.asp",
      "content": "The debt-to-equity (D/E) ratio is a financial metric used to evaluate a company's financial leverage by comparing its total liabilities to its shareholder equity. It measures a company's reliance on debt financing. A higher D/E ratio generally signals greater financial risk. The formula is: Total Liabilities / Total Shareholders' Equity. Information for this calculation is found on a company's balance sheet. The D/E ratio can be distorted by factors like retained earnings, intangible assets, and pension plan adjustments, often requiring further analysis. Analysts may modify the ratio to focus on long-term debt or assess it alongside other financial metrics. For example, Apple Inc. had a D/E ratio of 3.87 as of FY 2025. A D/E ratio below 1 is generally considered safe, while values of 2 or higher might be risky. Industries like utilities, consumer staples, and banking often have higher D/E ratios due to their capital needs and stable income streams. A negative D/E ratio indicates negative shareholder equity, signaling high risk. The D/E ratio can also be applied to personal finances to assess an individual's ability to manage debt. It's important to compare a company's D/E ratio with industry averages and competitors, as what constitutes a 'good' ratio varies by industry.",

2026-07-10T08:07:46.957539+00:00 | [SANCTIONS] Rationale:
An analysis of Grab Holdings Limited, its known shareholders, and its CEO Anthony Ping Yeow Tan was conducted using the provided screening data and web search results. No direct sanctions matches were identified for Grab Holdings Limited, Anthony Ping Yeow Tan, or any of the listed shareholders (such as SoftBank, Uber, Toyota, MUFG Bank, BlackRock, Morgan Stanley, Invesco, Wellington, Aspex, or Rothschild). Historical regulatory issues were noted, such as MUFG Bank's 2012 OFAC settlement and ongoing regulatory scrutiny of Morgan Stanley's wealth management division regarding client vetting, but these do not constitute active sanctions listings for the entities themselves. Additionally, Grab's shareholder structure is dominated by major institutional investors (SoftBank at ~10-18% and Uber at ~13.5-14%), which does not trigger the OFAC 50% rule for blocked-party ownership. No sanctioned parties were identified that would require shareholder review.

    {
      "title": "Disaster Recovery and Business Continuity Plans",
      "source_url": "https://cloudian.com/guides/disaster-recovery/disaster-recovery-and-business-continuity-plans/",
      "content": "Disaster Recovery (DR) plans, initially focused on natural disasters in the 1970s, evolved into Business Continuity (BC) plans from the 1980s onwards. DR narrowly focuses on restoring IT systems and data after a disruption, aiming to minimize downtime and data loss. A DR plan includes recovery objectives (RTO/RPO), backup procedures, recovery environments, roles, step-by-step recovery instructions, and testing. BC, on the other hand, is a broader, proactive process ensuring the entire organization continues operating during and after a crisis, covering business processes, human resources, partners, and suppliers. A BC plan details how a business will maintain financial viability, market position, reputation, and customers. A typical BC plan has 7 chapters: goals, budget, personnel, Business Impact Analysis, proactive strategies, immediate reactive strategies (including IT DR), and long-term reactive strategies. The key differences lie in scope (organization-wide vs. IT-focused), objective (maintaining operations vs. restoring systems), timing (proactive vs. reactive), business areas covered (people, processes, partners vs. technology, data), responsibility (executive/cross-functional vs. IT/security), metrics (business tolerance vs. technical targets), and output (operational vs. technical procedures). DR is a subset of BC, providing the technical foundation for maintaining operations. Cloudian offers solutions for data continuity, including low-cost disk-based storage up to 1.5 Petabytes and replication to cloud services like Amazon S3, Azure Blob Storage, or Google Cloud Storage.",

      "content": "Business Continuity Disaster Recovery (BCDR) is a process to help organizations resume normal operations after a disaster. While related, Business Continuity Plans (BCPs) are proactive, focusing on maintaining operations before, during, and immediately after a disaster, whereas Disaster Recovery Plans (DRPs) are reactive, focusing on IT system recovery post-incident. Both rely on Recovery Time Objective (RTO), the time to restore business processes, and Recovery Point Objective (RPO), the amount of data an organization can afford to lose. Key steps in BCDR planning include Business Impact Analysis (BIA) to identify risks and their potential impact, planning responses to threats, defining roles and responsibilities, and continuous testing and refinement. DRPs also involve risk analysis and asset inventory. Companies worldwide were projected to spend USD 219 billion on cybersecurity in 2023, a 12% increase from the previous year.       "title": "investopedia.com/terms/d/debtequityratio.asp",
      "source_url": "https://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/debtequityratio.asp",
      "content": "The debt-to-equity (D/E) ratio is a financial metric used to evaluate a company's financial leverage by comparing its total liabilities to its shareholder equity. It measures a company's reliance on debt financing. A higher D/E ratio generally signals greater financial risk. The formula is: Total Liabilities / Total Shareholders' Equity. Information for this calculation is found on a company's balance sheet. The D/E ratio can be distorted by factors like retained earnings, intangible assets, and pension plan adjustments, often requiring further analysis. Analysts may modify the ratio to focus on long-term debt or assess it alongside other financial metrics. For example, Apple Inc. had a D/E ratio of 3.87 as of FY 2025. A D/E ratio below 1 is generally considered safe, while values of 2 or higher might be risky. Industries like utilities, consumer staples, and banking often have higher D/E ratios due to their capital needs and stable income streams. A negative D/E ratio indicates negative shareholder equity, signaling high risk. The D/E ratio can also be applied to personal finances to assess an individual's ability to manage debt. It's important to compare a company's D/E ratio with industry averages and competitors, as what constitutes a 'good' ratio varies by industry.",
